Subdivisions {#Subdivisions}
----------------------------

\[[edit](/w/index.php?title=Lima&action=edit&section=30 "Edit section: Subdivisions")\]  
Main article: [List of districts of Lima](/wiki/List_of_districts_of_Lima "List of districts of Lima")
[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Metropolitan_Lima.png/220px-Metropolitan_Lima.png)](/wiki/File:Metropolitan_Lima.png) **Downtown Lima**   
[**Northern Lima**](/wiki/Cono_Norte "Cono Norte")   
[**Southern Lima**](/wiki/Cono_Sur_(Lima) "Cono Sur (Lima)")   
[**Eastern Lima**](/wiki/Cono_Este "Cono Este")

Lima is made up of thirty-one densely populated districts, each headed by a local mayor and the Mayor of Lima, whose authority extends to these and the twelve outer districts of the Lima province.

The city's [historic center](/wiki/Historic_Centre_of_Lima "Historic Centre of Lima") is located in the [Cercado de Lima](/wiki/Lima_District "Lima District") district, locally known as simply Lima, or as "El Centro" ("Center"). It is home to most of the vestiges the colonial past, the Presidential Palace (Spanish: *Palacio de Gobierno* ), the [Metropolitan Municipality and](/wiki/Metropolitan_Municipality_of_Lima "Metropolitan Municipality of Lima") (Spanish: *Consejo municipal metropolitano de Lima* ), [Chinatown](/wiki/Barrio_Chino_(Lima) "Barrio Chino (Lima)") and dozens of hotels, some operating and some defunct, that cater to the national and international elite.

The upscale [San Isidro District](/wiki/San_Isidro_District,_Lima "San Isidro District, Lima") is the city's financial center. It is home to politicians and celebrities. San Isidro has parks, including [Parque El Olivar](/wiki/El_Olivar,_Peru "El Olivar, Peru"), which is home to [olive](/wiki/Olive "Olive") trees imported from Spain during the seventeenth century. The Lima Golf Club, a prominent [golf club](/wiki/Country_club "Country club"), is located within the district.

Another upscale district is [Miraflores](/wiki/Miraflores_District,_Lima "Miraflores District, Lima"), which has luxury hotels, shops and restaurants. Miraflores has parks and green areas, more than most other districts. Larcomar, a shopping mall and entertainment center built on cliffs overlooking the Pacific Ocean, featuring bars, dance clubs, movie theaters, cafes, shops, boutiques and galleries, is also located in this district. Nightlife, shopping and entertainment center around [Parque Kennedy](/wiki/Miraflores_Central_Park "Miraflores Central Park"), a park in the heart of Miraflores.^[\[132\]](#cite_note-133)^{#cite_ref-133}

[La Molina](/wiki/La_Molina_District "La Molina District"), [San Borja](/wiki/San_Borja_District "San Borja District"), [Santiago de Surco](/wiki/Santiago_de_Surco "Santiago de Surco") -home to the American Embassy and the exclusive Club Polo Lima -- are the other three wealthy districts. The middle class districts in Lima are [Jesús María](/wiki/Jes%C3%BAs_Mar%C3%ADa_District,_Lima "Jesús María District, Lima"), [Lince](/wiki/Lince_District "Lince District"), [Magdalena del Mar](/wiki/Magdalena_del_Mar "Magdalena del Mar"), [Pueblo Libre](/wiki/Pueblo_Libre "Pueblo Libre"), [San Miguel](/wiki/San_Miguel_District,_Lima "San Miguel District, Lima") and [Barranco](/wiki/Barranco_District "Barranco District").

The most densely populated districts lie in Northern and Southern Lima, where the suburbs of the city begin (Spanish: [Cono Norte](/wiki/Cono_Norte "Cono Norte") and [Cono Sur](/wiki/Cono_Sur_(Lima) "Cono Sur (Lima)"), respectively) and they are mostly composed of Andean immigrants who arrived during the mid- and late- 20th century looking for a better life and economic opportunity, or as refugees of the country's internal conflict with the [Shining Path](/wiki/Shining_Path "Shining Path") during the late 1980s and early 1990s. In the case of Cono Norte (now called [Lima Norte](/wiki/Cono_Norte "Cono Norte")), shopping malls such as Megaplaza and Royal Plaza were built in the [Independencia](/wiki/Independencia_District,_Lima "Independencia District, Lima") district, on the border with the [Los Olivos District](/wiki/Los_Olivos_District "Los Olivos District") (the most residential neighborhood in the northern part). Most inhabitants are [middle](/wiki/Middle_class "Middle class") or [lower middle class](/wiki/Lower_middle_class "Lower middle class").

[Barranco](/wiki/Barranco_District "Barranco District"), which borders [Miraflores](/wiki/Miraflores_District,_Lima "Miraflores District, Lima") by the Pacific Ocean, is the city's bohemian district, home or once home of writers and intellectuals including [Mario Vargas Llosa](/wiki/Mario_Vargas_Llosa "Mario Vargas Llosa"), [Chabuca Granda](/wiki/Chabuca_Granda "Chabuca Granda") and [Alfredo Bryce Echenique](/wiki/Alfredo_Bryce_Echenique "Alfredo Bryce Echenique"). This district has restaurants, music venues called "peñas" featuring the traditional folk music of coastal Peru (in Spanish, "música criolla") and Victorian-style chalets. Along with Miraflores it serves as the home to the foreign nightlife scene.  

Education {#Education}
----------------------

\[[edit](/w/index.php?title=Lima&action=edit&section=31 "Edit section: Education")\]
[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Font_al_parque_Universitario_de_Lima_amb_el_Pante%C3%B3n_de_los_Pr%C3%B3ceres_al_fons.jpg/250px-Font_al_parque_Universitario_de_Lima_amb_el_Pante%C3%B3n_de_los_Pr%C3%B3ceres_al_fons.jpg)](/wiki/File:Font_al_parque_Universitario_de_Lima_amb_el_Pante%C3%B3n_de_los_Pr%C3%B3ceres_al_fons.jpg) Colonial [Casona and Chapel](/wiki/Casona_of_the_National_University_of_San_Marcos "Casona of the National University of San Marcos") of the [National University of San Marcos](/wiki/National_University_of_San_Marcos "National University of San Marcos"); it is the [oldest continuously operating university in the Americas](/wiki/List_of_colonial_universities_in_Hispanic_America "List of colonial universities in Hispanic America").

Home to universities, institutions and schools, Lima has the highest concentration of institutions of higher learning on the continent. In Lima is the oldest continuously operating university in the New World, [National University of San Marcos](/wiki/National_University_of_San_Marcos "National University of San Marcos"), founded in 1551.^[\[133\]](#cite_note-134)^{#cite_ref-134}

[Universidad Nacional de Ingeniería](/wiki/Universidad_Nacional_de_Ingenier%C3%ADa "Universidad Nacional de Ingeniería") (UNI) was founded in 1876 by Polish engineer [Edward Habich](/wiki/Edward_Habich "Edward Habich") and is the country's most important engineering school. Other public universities offer teaching and research, such as the [Universidad Nacional Federico Villarreal](/wiki/Universidad_Nacional_Federico_Villarreal "Universidad Nacional Federico Villarreal") (the second largest), the [Universidad Nacional Agraria La Molina](/wiki/Universidad_Nacional_Agraria_La_Molina "Universidad Nacional Agraria La Molina") (where ex-president [Alberto Fujimori](/wiki/Alberto_Fujimori "Alberto Fujimori") once taught) and the [National University of Callao](/wiki/National_University_of_Callao "National University of Callao").

The [Pontifical Catholic University of Peru](/wiki/Pontifical_Catholic_University_of_Peru "Pontifical Catholic University of Peru"), established in 1917, is the oldest private university. Other private institutions include [Universidad del Pacifico](/wiki/University_of_the_Pacific_(Peru) "University of the Pacific (Peru)"), [Universidad ESAN](/wiki/Universidad_ESAN "Universidad ESAN"), [Universidad de Lima](/wiki/Universidad_de_Lima "Universidad de Lima"), [Universidad de San Martín de Porres](/wiki/Universidad_de_San_Mart%C3%ADn_de_Porres "Universidad de San Martín de Porres"), [Universidad Peruana Cayetano Heredia](/wiki/Universidad_Peruana_Cayetano_Heredia "Universidad Peruana Cayetano Heredia"), [Universidad Cientifica del Sur](/wiki/Universidad_Cientifica_del_Sur "Universidad Cientifica del Sur"), [Universidad San Ignacio de Loyola](/wiki/Universidad_San_Ignacio_de_Loyola "Universidad San Ignacio de Loyola"), [Universidad Peruana de Ciencias Aplicadas](/wiki/Universidad_Peruana_de_Ciencias_Aplicadas "Universidad Peruana de Ciencias Aplicadas"), [Universidad Privada San Juan Bautista](/wiki/Universidad_Privada_San_Juan_Bautista "Universidad Privada San Juan Bautista") and Universidad Ricardo Palma.^[\[134\]](#cite_note-135)^{#cite_ref-135}

The city and has a total of 8,047 elementary and high schools, both public and private, which educate more than one and a half million students. The number of private schools is much greater than public schools (6,242 vs 1,805) while the average size of private schools is 100 for elementary and 130 for high school. Public schools average 400 students in elementary and 500 in high school.^[\[135\]](#cite_note-136)^{#cite_ref-136}
[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Ministerio_de_Educaci%C3%B3n%2C_Lima%2C_Peru.jpg/170px-Ministerio_de_Educaci%C3%B3n%2C_Lima%2C_Peru.jpg)](/wiki/File:Ministerio_de_Educaci%C3%B3n,_Lima,_Peru.jpg) Edificio Ministerio de Educación (Ministry of Education), San Borja

Lima has one of the country's highest levels of enrollment in high school and preschool. 86.1% of high school-age students are in school, vs the national average of 80.7%. In early childhood, the enrollment level in Lima is 84.7%, while the national average is 74.5%. Early childhood enrollment has improved by 12.1% since 2005. In elementary school, the enrollment in Lima is 90.7%, while the national average for this level is 92.9%.^[\[136\]](#cite_note-limacomovamos.org-137)^{#cite_ref-limacomovamos.org_137-0}

The dropout rate for Lima is lower than the national average, except for elementary school, which is higher. In Lima, the dropout rate in elementary is 1.3% and 5.7% in high school, while the national average is 1.2% in elementary and 8.3% in high school.^[\[136\]](#cite_note-limacomovamos.org-137)^{#cite_ref-limacomovamos.org_137-1}

In Peru, students grade second and fourth students take a test called "Evaluacion Censal de Estudiantes" (ECE). The test assesses skills in reading comprehension and math. Scores are grouped in three levels: Below level 1 means that students were not able to respond to even the most simple questions; level 1 means the students did not achieve the expected level in skills but could respond to simple questions; and level 2 means they achieved/exceeded the expected skills for their grade level. In 2012, 48.7% of students in Lima achieved level 2 in reading comprehension compared to 45.3% in 2011. In math, only 19.3% students achieved level 2, with 46.4% at level 1 and 34.2% less than level 1. Even though the results for Math are lower than for reading, in both subject areas performance increased in 2012 over 2011. The city performs much better than the national average in both disciplines.^[\[137\]](#cite_note-umc.minedu.gob.pe-138)^{#cite_ref-umc.minedu.gob.pe_138-0}

The educational system in Lima is organized under the authority of the "Direccion Regional de Educacion (DRE) de Lima Metropolitana", which is in turn divided into 7 sub-directions or "UGEL" (Unidad de Gestion Educativa Local): UGEL 01 (San Juan de Miraflores, Villa Maria del Triunfo, Villa El Salvador, Lurin, Pachacamac, San Bartolo, Punta Negra, Punta Hermosa, Pucusana, Santa Maria and Chilca), UGEL 02 (Rimac, Los Olivos, Independencia, Rimac and San Martin de Porres), UGEL 03 (Cercado, Lince, Breña, Pueblo Libre, San Miguel, Magdalena, Jesus Maria, La Victoria and San Isidro), UGEL 04 (Comas, Carabayllo, Puente Piedra, Santa Rosa and Ancon), UGEL 05 (San Juan de Lurigancho and El Agustino), UGEL 06 (Santa Anita, Lurigancho-Chosica, Vitarte, La Molina, Cieneguilla and Chaclacayo) and UGEL 07 (San Borja, San Luis, Surco, Surquillo, Miraflores, Barranco and Chorrillos).^[\[136\]](#cite_note-limacomovamos.org-137)^{#cite_ref-limacomovamos.org_137-2}

The UGELes with highest results on the ECE 2012 are UGEL 07 and 03 in both reading comprehension and math. UGEL 07 had 60.8% students achieving level 2 in reading comprehension and 28.6% students achieving level 2 in Math. UGEL 03 had 58.5% students achieve level 2 in reading comprehension and 24.9% students achieving level 2 in math. The lowest achieving UGELs are UGEL 01, 04 and 05.^[\[137\]](#cite_note-umc.minedu.gob.pe-138)^{#cite_ref-umc.minedu.gob.pe_138-1}

23% of men have completed university education in Lima, compared to 20% of women. Additionally, 16.2% of men have completed non-university higher education along with 17% of women. The average years of schooling in the city is 11.1 years (11.4 for men and 10.9 for women).^[\[52\]](#cite_note-inei.gob.pe-53)^{#cite_ref-inei.gob.pe_53-6}  

Transportation {#Transportation}
--------------------------------

\[[edit](/w/index.php?title=Lima&action=edit&section=32 "Edit section: Transportation")\]  
Main article: [Transport in Lima](/wiki/Transport_in_Lima "Transport in Lima")  

### Air {#Air}

\[[edit](/w/index.php?title=Lima&action=edit&section=33 "Edit section: Air")\]
[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Aeropuerto_de_Callao_-_Lima_en_Per%C3%BA_3.jpg/220px-Aeropuerto_de_Callao_-_Lima_en_Per%C3%BA_3.jpg)](/wiki/File:Aeropuerto_de_Callao_-_Lima_en_Per%C3%BA_3.jpg) [Jorge Chávez International Airport](/wiki/Jorge_Ch%C3%A1vez_International_Airport "Jorge Chávez International Airport")

Lima is served by [Jorge Chávez International Airport](/wiki/Jorge_Ch%C3%A1vez_International_Airport "Jorge Chávez International Airport"), located in [Callao](/wiki/Callao "Callao") (LIM). It is the country's largest airport hosting the largest number of domestic and international passengers. It serves as the fourth-largest hub in the Latin American air network. The airport is the hub for most Peruvian airlines, such as ATSA Airlines, [Star Perú](/wiki/Star_Per%C3%BA "Star Perú"), [JetSmart Perú](/wiki/JetSmart_Per%C3%BA "JetSmart Perú"), [Sky Airline Peru](/wiki/Sky_Airline_Peru "Sky Airline Peru") and the Peruvian flag carrier, [LATAM Perú](/wiki/LATAM_Airlines_Per%C3%BA "LATAM Airlines Perú"). Lima possesses five other airports: the Las Palmas Air Force Base, Collique Airport and runways in [Santa María del Mar](/wiki/Santa_Mar%C3%ADa_del_Mar_(Peru) "Santa María del Mar (Peru)"), [San Bartolo](/wiki/San_Bartolo_District "San Bartolo District") and [Chilca](/wiki/Chilca "Chilca").^[\[138\]](#cite_note-139)^{#cite_ref-139}

[Jorge Chávez International Airport](/wiki/Jorge_Ch%C3%A1vez_International_Airport "Jorge Chávez International Airport") is currently undergoing an expansion, with a new terminal being constructed along with an additional runway and multiple commercial areas. These new expansions will consist of the Lima Airport City. The airport will be the first airport city of Latin America and will increase the current airport capacity of 30 million to 40 million passengers. The expansion project will be complete on 30 March 2025.^[\[139\]](#cite_note-140)^{#cite_ref-140}  

### Road {#Road}

\[[edit](/w/index.php?title=Lima&action=edit&section=34 "Edit section: Road")\]  
Further information: [Highways in Peru](/wiki/Highways_in_Peru "Highways in Peru")
[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Panamericana_sur.jpg/220px-Panamericana_sur.jpg)](/wiki/File:Panamericana_sur.jpg) [South Panamerican Highway](/wiki/Peru_Highway_1 "Peru Highway 1")

Lima is a major stop on the [Pan-American Highway](/wiki/Pan-American_Highway "Pan-American Highway"). Because of its location on the country's central coast, Lima is an important junction in Peru's highway system. Three major highways originate in Lima.

* The [Northern Panamerican Highway](/wiki/Peru_Highway_1 "Peru Highway 1") extends more than 1,330 kilometers (830 mi) to the border with Ecuador connecting the northern districts with many major cities along the northern Peruvian coast.
* The Central Highway (Spanish: *Carretera Central* ) connects the eastern districts with cities in central Peru. The highway extends 860 kilometers (530 mi) with its terminus at [Pucallpa](/wiki/Pucallpa "Pucallpa") near Brazil.
* The [Southern Panamerican Highway](/wiki/Peru_Highway_1 "Peru Highway 1") connects the southern districts to the southern coast. The highway extends 1,450 kilometers (900 mi) to the border with Chile.

The city has a single major bus terminal next to the mall Plaza Norte. This bus station connects to national and international destinations. Other bus stations serve private bus companies around the city. In addition, informal bus stations are located in the south, center and north of the city.  

### Maritime {#Maritime}

\[[edit](/w/index.php?title=Lima&action=edit&section=35 "Edit section: Maritime")\]
[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Miraflores_Costa_Verde_Skyline_%28Lima%2C_Peru%29.jpg/285px-Miraflores_Costa_Verde_Skyline_%28Lima%2C_Peru%29.jpg)](/wiki/File:Miraflores_Costa_Verde_Skyline_(Lima,_Peru).jpg) Miraflores District on the coast

Lima's proximity to the port of [Callao](/wiki/Callao "Callao") allows Callao to act as the metropolitan area's major port and one of Latin America's largest. Callao hosts nearly all maritime transport for the metropolitan area. A small port in [Lurín](/wiki/Lur%C3%ADn_District "Lurín District") serves oil tankers due to a nearby refinery. Maritime transport inside Lima city limits is relatively insignificant compared to that of Callao.

The [Port of Chancay](/wiki/Port_of_Chancay "Port of Chancay") is currently the largest in South America with it commencing operations on 14 November 2024.  

### Rail {#Rail}

\[[edit](/w/index.php?title=Lima&action=edit&section=36 "Edit section: Rail")\]
[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Estaci%C3%B3n_de_Desamparados_o_Casa_de_la_Literatura_Peruana.jpg/220px-Estaci%C3%B3n_de_Desamparados_o_Casa_de_la_Literatura_Peruana.jpg)](/wiki/File:Estaci%C3%B3n_de_Desamparados_o_Casa_de_la_Literatura_Peruana.jpg) Desamparados Railway Station

Lima is connected to the Central Andean region by the [Ferrocarril Central Andino](/wiki/Ferrocarril_Central_Andino "Ferrocarril Central Andino") which runs from Lima through the departments of [Junín](/wiki/Jun%C3%ADn_Region "Junín Region"), [Huancavelica](/wiki/Huancavelica_Region "Huancavelica Region"), [Pasco](/wiki/Pasco_Region "Pasco Region") and [Huánuco](/wiki/Hu%C3%A1nuco_Region "Huánuco Region").^[\[140\]](#cite_note-141)^ Major cities along this line include [Huancayo](/wiki/Huancayo "Huancayo"), [La Oroya](/wiki/La_Oroya "La Oroya"), [Huancavelica](/wiki/Huancavelica "Huancavelica") and [Cerro de Pasco](/wiki/Cerro_de_Pasco "Cerro de Pasco"). Another inactive line runs from Lima northwards to the city of [Huacho](/wiki/Huacho "Huacho").^[\[141\]](#cite_note-142)^ [Commuter rail](/wiki/Commuter_rail "Commuter rail") services for Lima are planned as part of the larger [Tren de la Costa](/wiki/Tren_de_la_Costa_(Peru) "Tren de la Costa (Peru)") project. Lima's main railway station is the [Desamparados station](/wiki/Desamparados_station "Desamparados station").{#cite_ref-142}  

### Public {#Public}

\[[edit](/w/index.php?title=Lima&action=edit&section=37 "Edit section: Public")\]
[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Sistema_Integrado_de_Transporte_de_Lima.jpg/220px-Sistema_Integrado_de_Transporte_de_Lima.jpg)](/wiki/File:Sistema_Integrado_de_Transporte_de_Lima.jpg) Sistema Integrado de Transporte Bus System in [Arequipa Avenue](/wiki/Avenida_Arequipa "Avenida Arequipa") (Route 301)

Lima's road network is based mostly on large [divided avenues](/wiki/Dual_carriageway "Dual carriageway") rather than [freeways](/wiki/Controlled-access_highway "Controlled-access highway"). Lima operates a network of nine freeways -- the Via Expresa Paseo de la Republica, Via Expresa Javier Prado, Via Expresa Grau, Panamericana Norte, Panamericana Sur, Carretera Central, Via Expresa Callao, Autopista Chillon Trapiche and the Autopista Ramiro Priale.^[\[142\]](#cite_note-arqandina.com-143)^{#cite_ref-arqandina.com_143-0}

According to a 2012 survey, the majority of the population uses public or collective transportation (75.6%), while 12.3% uses a car, taxi or motorcycle.^[\[136\]](#cite_note-limacomovamos.org-137)^{#cite_ref-limacomovamos.org_137-3}

The urban transport system is composed of over 300 transit routes^[\[88\]](#cite_note-proinversion.gob.pe-89)^ that are served by buses, microbuses and combis.{#cite_ref-proinversion.gob.pe_89-1}

Taxis are mostly informal and unmetered; they are cheap but feature poor driving habits. Fares are agreed upon before the passenger enters the taxi. Taxis vary in size from small four-door compacts to large vans. They account for a large part of the car stock. In many cases they are just a private car with a taxi sticker on the windshield. Additionally, several companies provide on-call taxi service.^[\[143\]](#cite_note-144)^{#cite_ref-144}  

### Corredores Complementarios Bus System {#Corredores_Complementarios_Bus_System}

\[[edit](/w/index.php?title=Lima&action=edit&section=38 "Edit section: Corredores Complementarios Bus System")\]  
Main article: [Corredores complementarios](/wiki/Corredores_complementarios "Corredores complementarios")

The [Integrated Transport System](/wiki/Integrated_Transport_System "Integrated Transport System") (SIT), is a bus system developed by the local government to reorganize the current system of routes that has become chaotic. One of the main goals of the SIT is to reduce the number of urban routes, renew the bus fleet currently operating by many private companies and to reduce (and eventually replace) most "combis" from the city.

As of July 2020, SIT currently operates 16 routes: [San Martin de Porres](/wiki/San_Mart%C3%ADn_de_Porres_District "San Martín de Porres District") -- [Surco](/wiki/Santiago_de_Surco "Santiago de Surco") (107) [Ate](/wiki/Ate_District "Ate District") -- [San Miguel](/wiki/San_Miguel_District,_Lima "San Miguel District, Lima") (201, 202,204,206 and 209), [Rimac](/wiki/R%C3%ADmac_District "Rímac District") -- [Surco](/wiki/Santiago_de_Surco "Santiago de Surco") (301,302,303 and 306), [San Juan de Lurigancho](/wiki/San_Juan_de_Lurigancho "San Juan de Lurigancho") -- [Magdalena](/wiki/Magdalena_del_Mar_District "Magdalena del Mar District") (404,405,409,412), and [Downtown Lima](/wiki/Lima_District "Lima District") -- [San Miguel](/wiki/San_Miguel_District,_Lima "San Miguel District, Lima")(508)^\[*[citation needed](/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed "Wikipedia:Citation needed")*\]^  

### Colectivos {#Colectivos}

\[[edit](/w/index.php?title=Lima&action=edit&section=39 "Edit section: Colectivos")\]

[Colectivos](/wiki/Colectivo "Colectivo") render express service on some major roads. The colectivos signal their specific destination with a sign on their windshield. Their routes are not generally publicitized but are understood by frequent users. The cost is generally higher than public transport; however, they cover greater distances at greater speeds due to the lack of stops. This service is informal and is illegal.^[\[144\]](#cite_note-145)^ Some people in the periphery use so-called "mototaxis" for short distances.{#cite_ref-145}  

#### Metropolitan Transport System {#Metropolitan_Transport_System}

\[[edit](/w/index.php?title=Lima&action=edit&section=40 "Edit section: Metropolitan Transport System")\]
[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Lima_Metropoliano.jpg/220px-Lima_Metropoliano.jpg)](/wiki/File:Lima_Metropoliano.jpg) [El Metropolitano](/wiki/El_Metropolitano "El Metropolitano")

The Metropolitan Transport System or [El Metropolitano](/wiki/El_Metropolitano "El Metropolitano") is a new, integrated system, consisting of a network of buses that run in exclusive corridors under the Bus Rapid Transit system (BRT). The goal is to reduce passengers' commute times, protect the environment, provide improved security and overall quality of service. Metropolitano was executed with funds from the City of Lima and financing from the [Inter-American Development Bank](/wiki/Inter-American_Development_Bank "Inter-American Development Bank") and the [World Bank](/wiki/World_Bank "World Bank"). Metropolitana is the first BRT system to operate with natural gas, seeking to reduce air pollution.^[\[145\]](#cite_note-146)^ This system links the principal points of the Lima Metropolitan Area. The first phase of this project has 33 kilometers (21 mi) of line (north) to [Chorrillos](/wiki/Chorrillos_District "Chorrillos District") (south). It began commercial operations on 28 July 2010. Since 2014, Lima Council operates the "Sistema Integrado de Transporte Urbano" (Urban integrated transport system), which comprises buses over [Avenida Arequipa](/wiki/Avenida_Arequipa "Avenida Arequipa").^[\[146\]](#cite_note-147)^ By the end of 2012, the Metropolitano system counted 244 buses in its central routes and 179 buses in its feeding routes. Weekday use averages 437,148 passengers. Usage increased since 2011 by 28.2% for weekdays, 29.1% for Saturdays and 33.3% for Sundays.^[\[136\]](#cite_note-limacomovamos.org-137)^{#cite_ref-limacomovamos.org_137-4}
[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Linea_1_del_Metro_de_Lima.jpg/220px-Linea_1_del_Metro_de_Lima.jpg)](/wiki/File:Linea_1_del_Metro_de_Lima.jpg) [Lima Metro](/wiki/Lima_Metro "Lima Metro")  

#### Metro {#Metro}

\[[edit](/w/index.php?title=Lima&action=edit&section=41 "Edit section: Metro")\]

The [Lima Metro](/wiki/Lima_Metro "Lima Metro") is expected to have six lines. As of 2024, there are two lines running and a third under construction. The other lines are in the ministry of transportation investments portfolio for the next 5 years.

Line 1 has twenty six passenger stations, with an average separation of 1.2 kilometres (3,900 ft). It begins in the Industrial Park of Villa El Salvador, south of the city, continuing on to Av. Pachacútec in Villa María del Triunfo and then to Av. Los Héroes in San Juan de Miraflores. Afterwards, it continues through Av. Tomás Marsano in Surco to reach Ov. Los Cabitos, to Av. Aviación and then cross the river Rimac to finish, after almost 35 km (22 mi), in the east of the capital in [San Juan de Lurigancho](/wiki/San_Juan_de_Lurigancho "San Juan de Lurigancho"). The system operates 24 trains, each with six wagons. Each wagon has the capacity to transport 233 people. The metro system began operating in December 2012 and transported 78,224 people on average on a daily basis.^[\[136\]](#cite_note-limacomovamos.org-137)^{#cite_ref-limacomovamos.org_137-5}

Line 2 of the metro has a first stage already working, the expansion of the line it's being delivered in 2026, along with a portion of line 4 that will be connecting the airport to the metro system. Line 3 and expansion of line 4 are now in the pre bid stage to be done in 2025.  

### Other transportation issues {#Other_transportation_issues}

\[[edit](/w/index.php?title=Lima&action=edit&section=42 "Edit section: Other transportation issues")\]
[![avenue](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Intensa_Congesti%C3%B3n_vehicular_en_Lima.jpg/200px-Intensa_Congesti%C3%B3n_vehicular_en_Lima.jpg)](/wiki/File:Intensa_Congesti%C3%B3n_vehicular_en_Lima.jpg) [Traffic Jam](/wiki/Traffic_congestion "Traffic congestion") in Javier Prado Avenue

Lima has high traffic congestion, especially at peak hours. 1.397 million vehicles were in use by the end of 2012. The region operates 65.3% of the cars in the country.^[\[136\]](#cite_note-limacomovamos.org-137)^{#cite_ref-limacomovamos.org_137-6}

The Ministry of Economy and Finance (MEF) offered economic incentives for municipalities to implement bicycle routes in their districts. Recreational bike lanes can be found in 39 districts. The Proyecto Especial Metropolitano de Transporte No Motorizado (PEMTNM) estimates that more than a million and a half people used the bike lanes in 2012. The bike lanes ran for 71 km (44 mi). They estimate that the use of the bike lanes prevented the emission of 526 tons of carbon dioxide in 2012.^[\[136\]](#cite_note-limacomovamos.org-137)^{#cite_ref-limacomovamos.org_137-7}

San Borja district was the first to implement a bike-share program called San Borja en Bici. It supplied 200 bicycles and six stations across the district (two of them connecting with the Metro). By December 2012, the program had 2,776 subscribers.^[\[147\]](#cite_note-148)^{#cite_ref-148}  

Challenges {#Challenges}
------------------------

\[[edit](/w/index.php?title=Lima&action=edit&section=43 "Edit section: Challenges")\]  

### Environment {#Environment}

\[[edit](/w/index.php?title=Lima&action=edit&section=44 "Edit section: Environment")\]
[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Parapentes_Miraflores_Lima_PE.jpg/220px-Parapentes_Miraflores_Lima_PE.jpg)](/wiki/File:Parapentes_Miraflores_Lima_PE.jpg) Paragliding in Miraflores  

#### Air {#Air_2}

\[[edit](/w/index.php?title=Lima&action=edit&section=45 "Edit section: Air")\]

Lima suffers the most from air pollution. The microscopic sediment contained within engine exhaust and industrial emissions floats in the air for extended periods of time, either continuing as air pollution or eventually coming to settle as dust upon different urban surfaces. The fine particles are the most dangerous, as if inhaled, are able to damage the human [respiratory system](/wiki/Respiratory_system "Respiratory system"). The recommended limit of these particles by the [World Health Organization](/wiki/World_Health_Organization "World Health Organization") is 5 tons/km2/month. In February 2014, Lima recorded an average of 15.2 tons/km2. The two districts with the highest concentration of sedimentary dust are El Agustino (46.1 tons/km2) and Independencia (25.5 tons/km2) in February 2014.^[\[136\]](#cite_note-limacomovamos.org-137)^^[\[148\]](#cite_note-ReferenceA-149)^{#cite_ref-ReferenceA_149-0}

Lima has built [billboards](/wiki/Billboard "Billboard") which serve as [air purifiers](/wiki/Air_purifier "Air purifier").^[\[149\]](#cite_note-150)^{#cite_ref-150}
[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Fiume_Rimac.JPG/200px-Fiume_Rimac.JPG)](/wiki/File:Fiume_Rimac.JPG) Rimac river  

#### Water {#Water}

\[[edit](/w/index.php?title=Lima&action=edit&section=46 "Edit section: Water")\]

The permissible limit of lead in the water supply is 0.05 milligrams per liter, according to the Norm ITINTEC. In January 2014, the concentration of minerals in water treatment facilities of [SEDAPAL](/wiki/SEDAPAL "SEDAPAL") was 0.051 iron, 0.005 lead, 0.0012 cadmium and 0.0810 aluminum. These values increased 15.9% and 33.3% in iron and cadmium with respect to January 2013 and a decrease of 16.7% and 12.4% in lead and aluminum. The values are within the recommended limits.^[\[148\]](#cite_note-ReferenceA-149)^{#cite_ref-ReferenceA_149-1}  

#### Solid waste {#Solid_waste}

\[[edit](/w/index.php?title=Lima&action=edit&section=47 "Edit section: Solid waste")\]

The amount of solid waste produced per capita in Lima is about 0.7 kg (2 lb) per day. In 2012, each resident produced 273.36 kg (603 lb) of solid waste. The district municipalities only collect about 67% of the solid waste they generate. The rest ends up in informal landfills, rivers, or the ocean. Three municipalities recycle 20% or more of their waste.^[\[52\]](#cite_note-inei.gob.pe-53)^{#cite_ref-inei.gob.pe_53-7}

**COVID-19**

Lima has a population of about 10 million people. This equates to one-third of the overall population of the nation. Lima was subjected to a lengthy period of confinement or quarantine that lasted more than four months. However, in May 2020, the process of restoring activities began as part of the national government's economic reactivation strategy.^[\[150\]](#cite_note-:02-151)^^[\[151\]](#cite_note-:3-152)^{#cite_ref-:3_152-0}

Lima's [green recovery](/wiki/Green_recovery "Green recovery") is centred on bringing services closer to the most vulnerable people, with the goal of creating a green, wealthy, and equitable city for all. The municipality has an aim of implementing sustainable infrastructure to limit the development of [COVID-19](/wiki/COVID-19 "COVID-19") through the execution of 46 km of rising bike routes.^[\[150\]](#cite_note-:02-151)^^[\[151\]](#cite_note-:3-152)^{#cite_ref-:3_152-1}

Lima is one of over 10,000 cities from all around the world that make up the [Global Covenant of Mayors.](/wiki/Global_Covenant_of_Mayors_for_Climate_%26_Energy "Global Covenant of Mayors for Climate & Energy")^[\[150\]](#cite_note-:02-151)^^[\[152\]](#cite_note-153)^{#cite_ref-153}  

### Access to basic services {#Access_to_basic_services}

\[[edit](/w/index.php?title=Lima&action=edit&section=48 "Edit section: Access to basic services")\]

In Lima, 93% of households have access to water supply in their homes. In addition, 92% of homes connect with sewage systems. 99.6% of homes have grid electric service. Although most households have water and sewage systems, some are available for only a few hours a day.^[\[136\]](#cite_note-limacomovamos.org-137)^{#cite_ref-limacomovamos.org_137-9}

One and a half million inhabitants have no access to drinking water. The water problem mainly affects the outlying areas of the capital. The story of water in Lima is one of inequality. In the most affluent districts, per capita water consumption stands at 350 liters per day. In the poorest neighborhoods, it's between 20 and 70 liters a day.^[\[153\]](#cite_note-154)^^[\[154\]](#cite_note-155)^{#cite_ref-155}  

### Security {#Security}

\[[edit](/w/index.php?title=Lima&action=edit&section=49 "Edit section: Security")\]
[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Lima_from_above.jpg/260px-Lima_from_above.jpg)](/wiki/File:Lima_from_above.jpg) San Isidro, Lima from above

The perception of security varies by district. For example, San Isidro has the lowest perception of insecurity (21.4%), while Rimac has the highest perception of insecurity (85%), according to a 2012 survey. The five districts with the lowest perception of insecurity are San Isidro, San Borja, Miraflores, La Molina and Jesus Maria. The districts with the highest perception of insecurity are Rimac, San Juan de Miraflores, La Victoria, Comas and Ate.^[\[155\]](#cite_note-ciudadnuestra.org-156)^{#cite_ref-ciudadnuestra.org_156-0}

Overall, 40% of the population in Lima above 15 years old has been a crime victim. The younger population (ages 15 to 29 years old) has the highest victimization rate (47.9%).^[\[52\]](#cite_note-inei.gob.pe-53)^ In 2012, citizens reported thefts (47.9%): in homes or establishments (19.4%), robbery or attack (14.9%), gang aggression (5.7%), among others in lesser frequency. The districts with the highest level of victimization are Rimac, El Agustino, Villa El Salvador, San Juan de Lurigancho and Los Olivos. The safest districts by level of victimization are Lurin, Lurigancho-Chosica, San Borja, Magdalena and Surquillo. These districts do not necessarily correspond to the districts with highest or lowest perception of insecurity.^[\[155\]](#cite_note-ciudadnuestra.org-156)^{#cite_ref-ciudadnuestra.org_156-1}
[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Miraflores_2023.jpg/220px-Miraflores_2023.jpg)](/wiki/File:Miraflores_2023.jpg) [Miraflores District](/wiki/Miraflores_District,_Lima "Miraflores District, Lima"), among the safest areas of Lima

While the police force is nationally controlled and funded, each district in Lima has a community policing structure called Serenazgo. The quantity of Serenazgos officials and resources varies by district. For example, Villa Maria del Triunfo has 5,785 citizens per official. Twenty-two districts in Lima have a ratio above 1000 citizens per Serenazgo official, while 14 districts have ratios below 200 citizens per official, including Miraflores with 119 and San Isidro with 57.^[\[52\]](#cite_note-inei.gob.pe-53)^{#cite_ref-inei.gob.pe_53-9}

The satisfaction with the Serenazgos also varies greatly by district. The highest satisfaction rates can be found in San Isidro (88.3%), Miraflores (81.6%), San Borja (77%) and Surco (75%). The lowest satisfaction rates can be found in Villa Maria del Triunfo (11%), San Juan de Miraflores (14.8%), Rimac (16.3%) and La Victoria (20%).^[\[155\]](#cite_note-ciudadnuestra.org-156)^{#cite_ref-ciudadnuestra.org_156-2}  

#### Wall of Shame {#Wall_of_Shame}

\[[edit](/w/index.php?title=Lima&action=edit&section=50 "Edit section: Wall of Shame")\]

The mid-20th century saw the rural population (between 600,000 and 1 million people) taking refuge in Lima, especially during the [Peruvian conflict](/wiki/Peruvian_conflict "Peruvian conflict").^[\[7\]](#cite_note-vatik-8)^ The new arrivals, often very poor, erected hastily built shacks. Some residents of these shantytown neighborhoods have acquired property titles, but urban planning remains largely non-existent. In response, a number of wealthy neighbourhoods built their own security barriers starting in 1985, citing security concerns.^[\[7\]](#cite_note-vatik-8)^ By 2019, some segments were up to 3 metres high and included [barbed wire](/wiki/Barbed_wire "Barbed wire"),^[\[156\]](#cite_note-157)^ having a total length of ten kilometres (6.2 mi).^[\[157\]](#cite_note-158)^^[\[158\]](#cite_note-NACLA-159)^{#cite_ref-NACLA_159-0}
[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Muro_de_la_verguenza_-_Wall_of_shame.jpg/220px-Muro_de_la_verguenza_-_Wall_of_shame.jpg)](/wiki/File:Muro_de_la_verguenza_-_Wall_of_shame.jpg) The wall divides the district of San Juan de Miraflores (one of the most chaotic, dangerous and poor) from the district of Santiago de Surco (one of the safest and most luxurious) through a hill (the urbanized part is San Juan de Miraflores and the non-urbanized part is Santiago de Surco)

Critics have used the term **wall of shame** ([Spanish](/wiki/Spanish_language "Spanish language"): *muro de la vergüenza* ) to refer to the barriers, which are the subject of tension, especially as many inhabitants of the poorer area cross the wall everyday to work in the neighbouring affluent areas, usually as gardeners or domestic workers.^[\[159\]](#cite_note-Atlantic-160)^ The wall was partially demolished in 2023 in a number of places.^[\[160\]](#cite_note-161)^^[\[7\]](#cite_note-vatik-8)^^[\[8\]](#cite_note-Reuters-9)^{#cite_ref-Reuters_9-1}
